'To find the constant term in the expansion of $\\left(10x^3-\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^{5}$, we can use the binomial theorem. The general form of the binomial theorem states that for any real numbers a and b, and any non-negative integer n, the expansion of $(a + b)^n$ is given by:\n\n$(a + b)^n = \\binom{n}{0}a^{n}b^{0} + \\binom{n}{1}a^{n-1}b^{1} + \\binom{n}{2}a^{n-2}b^{2} + ... + \\binom{n}{n-1}a^{1}b^{n-1} + \\binom{n}{n}a^{0}b^{n}$\n\nwhere $\\binom{n}{k}$ represents the binomial coefficient "n choose k".\n\nIn this case, we have $a = 10x^3$ and $b = -\\frac{1}{2x^2}$, and we are looking for the constant term when expanded to the 5th power.\n\nTo find the constant term, we need to look for the term where the powers of $x$ cancel out. This occurs when the powers of $x$ in $a$ and $b$ combine to give a power of $x^0$.\n\nFor the constant term, the powers of $x$ in $a$ and $b$ must satisfy the equation $3m - 2n = 0$, where m is the power of $x$ in $a$ and n is the power of $x$ in $b$.\n\nIn this case, the constant term will occur when $3m - 2n = 0$. Solving this equation gives $m = \\frac{2}{3}n$.\n\nSince we are looking for the constant term in the expansion to the 5th power, we need to find the value of n that satisfies $m + n = 5$.\n\nSubstitute $m = \\frac{2}{3}n$ into $m + n = 5$ to get $\\frac{2}{3}n + n = 5$. Solving for n gives $n = 3$.\n\nTherefore, the constant term in the expansion of $\\left(10x^3-\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^{5}$ occurs when $m = 2$ and $n = 3$. Plugging these values into the binomial theorem formula, we get:\n\n$\\binom{5}{2}(10x^3)^{2}\\left(-\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^{3} = 10\\binom{5}{2}x^{6}\\left(-\\frac{1}{8}x^{-6}\\right) = -\\frac{5}{4} \\binom{5}{2}$\n\nCalculating $\\binom{5}{2}$ gives $\\binom{5}{2} = \\frac{5!}{2!(5-2)!} = 10$.\n\nTherefore, the constant term in the expansion of $\\left(10x^3-\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^{5}$ is $-\\frac{5}{4} \\times 10 = -\\frac{50}{4} = -12.5$.'
'Given the matrix $\\mathbf{A} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 7 \\\\ -1 & -2 \\end{pmatrix}$, we are asked to find the value of $\\mathbf{A}^{27} + \\mathbf{A}^{31} + \\mathbf{A}^{40}$.\n\nTo find the powers of the matrix $\\mathbf{A}$, we can use the concept of diagonalization. Diagonalization of a matrix involves finding a matrix $\\mathbf{P}$ such that $\\mathbf{P}^{-1}\\mathbf{A}\\mathbf{P} = \\mathbf{D}$, where $\\mathbf{D}$ is a diagonal matrix. The diagonal elements of $\\mathbf{D}$ will be the eigenvalues of $\\mathbf{A}$.\n\nFirst, we find the eigenvalues of $\\mathbf{A}$ by solving the characteristic equation $|\\mathbf{A} - \\lambda\\mathbf{I}| = 0$, where $\\lambda$ is the eigenvalue and $\\mathbf{I}$ is the identity matrix.\n\nThe characteristic equation is:\n$|\\begin{pmatrix} 3-\\lambda & 7 \\\\ -1 & -2-\\lambda \\end{pmatrix}| = 0$\n$(3-\\lambda)(-2-\\lambda) - 7(-1) = 0$\n$-\\lambda^2 - \\lambda + 6 - 7 = 0$\n$-\\lambda^2 - \\lambda - 1 = 0$\n\nSolving the quadratic equation, we get the eigenvalues:\n$\\lambda_1 = \\frac{-(-1) + \\sqrt{(-1)^2 - 4(-1)(-1)}}{2(-1)} = \\frac{1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}$\n$\\lambda_2 = \\frac{-(-1) - \\sqrt{(-1)^2 - 4(-1)(-1)}}{2(-1)} = \\frac{1 - \\sqrt{5}}{2}$\n\nNext, we find the eigenvectors corresponding to each eigenvalue by solving the system of equations $(\\mathbf{A} - \\lambda\\mathbf{I})\\mathbf{v} = \\mathbf{0}$, where $\\mathbf{v}$ is the eigenvector.\n\nFor $\\lambda_1 = \\frac{1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}$:\n$(\\mathbf{A} - \\lambda_1\\mathbf{I})\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 - \\frac{1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2} & 7 \\\\ -1 & -2 - \\frac{1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2} \\end{pmatrix}\\begin{pmatrix} v_1 \\\\ v_2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}$\n\nSolving the system of equations, we get an eigenvector corresponding to $\\lambda_1$.\n\nFor $\\lambda_2 = \\frac{1 - \\sqrt{5}}{2}$:\n$(\\mathbf{A} - \\lambda_2\\mathbf{I})\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 - \\frac{1 - \\sqrt{5}}{2} & 7 \\\\ -1 & -2 - \\frac{1 - \\sqrt{5}}{2} \\end{pmatrix}\\begin{pmatrix} v_1 \\\\ v_2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}$\n\nSolving the system of equations, we get an eigenvector corresponding to $\\lambda_2$.\n\nOnce we have the eigenvectors, we can form the matrix $\\mathbf{P}$ using the eigenvectors as columns. Then, we find $\\mathbf{P}^{-1}$ and $\\mathbf{D}$.\n\nAfter diagonalizing the matrix $\\mathbf{A}$, we can find $\\mathbf{A}^{27}$, $\\mathbf{A}^{31}$, and $\\mathbf{A}^{40}$ by raising the diagonal matrix $\\mathbf{D}$ to the corresponding powers.\n\nFinally, we can find the value of $\\mathbf{A}^{27} + \\mathbf{A}^{31} + \\mathbf{A}^{40}$ by converting the diagonalized matrices back to the original form using $\\mathbf{P}$ and summing the matrices.'

'To find the largest integer that satisfies the given conditions, we can use the Chinese Remainder Theorem. \n\nLet the integer we are looking for be $x$. According to the conditions given:\n1. $x \\equiv 5 \\pmod{7}$\n2. $x \\equiv 10 \\pmod{11}$\n3. $x \\equiv 10 \\pmod{13}$\n\nFrom the first condition, we can express $x$ as $x = 7a + 5$, where $a$ is an integer.\nSubstitute this into the second condition: $7a + 5 \\equiv 10 \\pmod{11}$\nThis simplifies to $7a \\equiv 5 \\pmod{11}$\nSolving this congruence, we find $a \\equiv 8 \\pmod{11}$\nSo, $a = 11b + 8$, where $b$ is an integer.\nSubstitute this back into $x$: $x = 7(11b + 8) + 5 = 77b + 61$\n\nNow, substitute $x$ into the third condition: $77b + 61 \\equiv 10 \\pmod{13}$\nThis simplifies to $2b \\equiv 12 \\pmod{13}$\nSolving this congruence, we find $b \\equiv 6 \\pmod{13}$\nSo, $b = 13c + 6$, where $c$ is an integer.\nSubstitute this back into $x$: $x = 77(13c + 6) + 61 = 1001c + 613$\n\nTo find the largest integer less than $2010$ that satisfies the conditions, we need to find the largest possible value of $c$ such that $1001c + 613 < 2010$.\nSolving this inequality, we get $c < \\frac{397}{1001}$\nTherefore, the largest integer less than $2010$ that satisfies the conditions is when $c = 0$, which gives $x = 613$.\n\nTherefore, the largest integer less than $2010$ that has a remainder of $5$ when divided by $7$, a remainder of $10$ when divided by $11$, and a remainder of $10$ when divided by $13$ is $\\boxed{613}$.'

"From the hint, we know that the probability of exactly two out of $n$ fair 6-sided dice showing a number other than 1 is $\\frac{25}{216}$. \n\nLet's break down the probability calculation:\n- The probability of getting a number other than 1 on a single die is $\\frac{5}{6}$.\n- The probability of getting a 1 on a single die is $\\frac{1}{6}$.\n- The probability of getting a number other than 1 on two dice and a 1 on the rest is $\\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^2 \\cdot \\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right)^{n-2}$.\n- The total number of ways to choose 2 out of $n$ dice is $\\binom{n}{2}$.\n- The total number of outcomes when rolling $n$ dice is $6^n$.\n\nTherefore, the probability of exactly two out of $n$ fair 6-sided dice showing a number other than 1 can be calculated as:\n\\[\\binom{n}{2} \\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^2 \\cdot \\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right)^{n-2} = \\frac{25}{216}\\]\n\nSolving this equation will give us the value of $n$. 'To find the modular inverse of $17$ modulo $83$, we need to find an integer $x$ such that $17x \\equiv 1 \\pmod{83}$.\n\nWe can use the Extended Euclidean Algorithm to find the modular inverse. The algorithm proceeds as follows:\n\n1. We start by expressing $83$ as $83 = 17 \\cdot 4 + 15$.\n2. Next, we express $17$ as $17 = 15 \\cdot 1 + 2$.\n3. We then express $15$ as $15 = 2 \\cdot 7 + 1$.\n4. Finally, we express $2$ as $2 = 1 \\cdot 2 + 0$.\n\nNow, we backtrack using the equations above to express $1$ as a linear combination of $83$ and $17$:\n\n1. From $1 = 15 - 2 \\cdot 7$, substitute $2 = 17 - 15$:\n   $1 = 15 - (17 - 15) \\cdot 7$\n   $1 = 15 \\cdot 8 - 17 \\cdot 7$\n\n2. Substitute $15 = 83 - 17 \\cdot 4$:\n   $1 = (83 - 17 \\cdot 4) \\cdot 8 - 17 \\cdot 7$\n   $1 = 83 \\cdot 8 - 17 \\cdot 32 - 17 \\cdot 7$\n   $1 = 83 \\cdot 8 - 17 \\cdot 39$\n\nTherefore, the modular inverse of $17$ modulo $83$ is $8$.'
'To find the product of the $y$-coordinates of all distinct solutions $(x,y)$ for the two equations $y=x^2-8$ and $y^2=-5x+44, we first need to find the solutions to these equations.\n\n1. From the equation $y=x^2-8$, we can substitute $y$ into the second equation to get:\n$(x^2-8)^2=-5x+44$\nExpanding and simplifying, we get:\n$x^4 - 16x^2 + 64 = -5x + 44$\nRearranging terms, we get:\n$x^4 - 16x^2 + 5x + 20 = 0$\nThis is a quartic equation that can be factored as:\n$(x^2 - 5)(x^2 - 4) = 0$\nThis gives us solutions $x=\\pm\\sqrt{5}$ and $x=\\pm2$.\n\n2. 'Given that $P(x)$ is a monic polynomial of degree 3, we can express $P(x)$ as $P(x) = x^3 + ax^2 + bx + c$.\n\nSince $P(x)$ has remainder $R(x)$ when divided by $(x - 1)(x - 4)$, we can express $R(x)$ as $R(x) = dx + e$ for some constants $d$ and $e$. This gives us the equation:\n\n$P(x) = (x - 1)(x - 4)Q(x) + dx + e$\n\nSubstitute $P(x) = x^3 + ax^2 + bx + c$ into the equation above:\n\n$x^3 + ax^2 + bx + c = (x - 1)(x - 4)Q(x) + dx + e$\n\nExpanding the right side and comparing coefficients, we get:\n\n$a = d + 5$\n\n$b = -4d + e + 4$\n\n$c = -5e$\n\nSince $P(x)$ has remainder $2R(x)$ when divided by $(x - 2)(x - 3)$, we can express $2R(x)$ as $2(dx + e) = 2dx + 2e$. This gives us the equation:\n\n$P(x) = (x - 2)(x - 3)S(x) + 2dx + 2e$\n\nSubstitute $P(x) = x^3 + ax^2 + bx + c$ into the equation above:\n\n$x^3 + ax^2 + bx + c = (x - 2)(x - 3)S(x) + 2dx + 2e$\n\nExpanding the right side and comparing coefficients, we get:\n\n$a = 2d + 5$\n\n$b = -5d + 2e + 6$\n\n$c = -6e$\n\nFrom the equations above, we can solve for $d$ and $e$:\n\n$d = 1, e = -1$\n\nTherefore, $a = 6, b = -1, c = 5$.\n\nSo, $P(x) = x^3 + 6x^2 - x + 5$.\n\nGiven that $P(0) = 5$, we have:\n\n$P(0) = 5 = 0 + 0 - 0 + 5$\n\nNow, to find $P(5)$, we substitute $x = 5$ into $P(x)$:\n\n$P(5) = 5^3 + 6(5)^2 - 5 + 5 = 125 + 150 - 5 + 5 = 275$\n\nTherefore, $P(5) = 275$.'

Since there are an odd number of integers (27), the median will be the 14th number in the sequence. \n\nTo find the 14th number, we can use the formula for the nth term of an arithmetic sequence: $a_n = a_1 + (n-1)d$, where $a_n$ is the nth term, $a_1$ is the first term, $n$ is the position of the term, and $d$ is the common difference.\n\nIn this case, the first term $a_1$ can be found by subtracting 13 (since we want the 14th term) times the common difference from the average:\n$a_1 = 81 - 13d$\n\nWe know that the sum of the 27 integers is $3^7$, so we can also express the sum in terms of the first and last terms of the sequence:\n$3^7 = 27/2 * (a_1 + a_{27})$\n\nSubstitute $a_1 = 81 - 13d$ and $a_{27} = 81 + 13d$ into the equation above and solve for $d$:\n$2187 = 27/2 * (81 - 13d + 81 + 13d)$\n$2187 = 27/2 * 162$\n$2187 = 2187$\n\nThis confirms that our initial assumption of the average being 81 is correct. Therefore, the median of the 27 consecutive positive integers is the 14th term, which is $81$.'

"Given the information provided in the question, we have a monic polynomial $P(x)$ of degree 3 with remainder $R(x)$ when divided by $(x - 1)(x - 4)$ and remainder $2R(x)$ when divided by $(x - 2)(x - 3)$. We are also given that $P(0) = 5$.\n\nLet's denote the polynomial $P(x)$ as:\n$P(x) = (x - 1)(x - 4)Q_1(x) + R(x)$\n$P(x) = (x - 2)(x - 3)Q_2(x) + 2R(x)$\n\nwhere $Q_1(x)$ and $Q_2(x)$ are the quotients when dividing $P(x)$ by $(x - 1)(x - 4)$ and $(x - 2)(x - 3)$ respectively.\n\nGiven that $P(0) = 5$, we can substitute $x = 0$ into the first equation:\n$P(0) = (0 - 1)(0 - 4)Q_1(0) + R(0) = -4Q_1(0) + R(0) = 5$\n\nSince $R(x)$ is the remainder when dividing $P(x)$ by $(x - 1)(x - 4)$, it must be of degree less than 2. Therefore, $R(x)$ is a constant term, let's denote it as $c$.\n\nFrom the equation $-4Q_1(0) + c = 5$, we can solve for $c$:\n$-4Q_1(0) + c = 5$\n$-4(0) + c = 5$\n$c = 5$\n\nTherefore, the remainder $R(x)$ is a constant term $c = 5$.\n\nNow, let's find the polynomial $P(x)$ using the information given:\n$P(x) = (x - 1)(x - 4)Q_1(x) + 5$\n$P(x) = (x - 2)(x - 3)Q_2(x) + 10$\n\nSince $P(x)$ is a monic polynomial of degree 3, we can write it as:\n$P(x) = x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 5$\n\nSubstitute $P(x)$ into the equations above and expand:\n$x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 5 = (x - 1)(x - 4)Q_1(x) + 5$\n$x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 5 = (x - 2)(x - 3)Q_2(x) + 10$\n\nBy comparing coefficients, we can solve for $a$ and $b$.\n\nFinally, to find $P(5)$, substitute $x = 5$ into the polynomial $P(x)$:\n$P(5) = 5^3 + a(5)^2 + b(5) + 5$\n\nAfter finding the values of $a$ and $b$ from the equations above, you can calculate $P(5)$."
"To find the number of real values of $x$ for which $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer, we need to first determine the values of $x$ that make $120-\\sqrt{x}$ a perfect square.\n\nLet $y = \\sqrt{x}$. Then, we have $120 - y = z^2$, where $z$ is an integer. Rearranging, we get $y = 120 - z^2$. Since $y = \\sqrt{x}$, $x = y^2 = (120 - z^2)^2$.\n\nFor $x$ to be a real number, we must have $120 - z^2 \\geq 0$, which implies $z^2 \\leq 120$. The possible values for $z$ are $0, \\pm 1, \\pm 2, ..., \\pm 10$. This gives us a total of $2(10) + 1 = 21$ possible values for $z$.\n\nTherefore, there are 21 real values of $x$ for which $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer.\n\nNow, let's move on to the Senate committee members sitting around a circular table. If all the members of each party sit next to each other, we can consider each party as a single entity. This reduces the problem to arranging two entities (parties) around a circular table.\n\nThe number of ways to arrange $n$ distinct objects around a circular table is $(n-1)!$. In this case, we have 2 entities (parties), so the number of ways to arrange them around the table is $(2-1)! = 1! = 1$.\n\nTherefore, there is only 1 way for the Senate committee members to sit around a circular table if all the members of each party sit next to each other."

Since the sum of the numbers on each pair of opposite faces is 7, the probability of obtaining a sum of 7 is:\n$p \\cdot q = 47/288$\n\nSubstitute $p + q = 1/3$ into the equation above:\n$p \\cdot (1/3 - p) = 47/288$\n$p/3 - p^2 = 47/288$\n$p^2 - p/3 + 47/288 = 0$\n\nSolving this quadratic equation, we get:\n$p = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 - 4 \\cdot \\frac{47}{288}}}{2}$\n$p = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{47}{72}}}{2}$\n$p = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{25}{72}}}{2}$\n$p = \\frac{1 \\pm \\frac{5}{6}}{2}$\n\nSince $p > 1/6$, we take the positive solution:\n$p = \\frac{1 + 5/6}{2} = \\frac{11}{12}$\n\nTherefore, the value of $m + n = 11 + 12 = \\boxed{23}$."

'To find the probability that the points lie on the same straight line, we need to consider the different ways in which this can happen.\n\nThere are a total of $\\binom{9}{3} = 84$ ways to choose 3 points from the 3x3 grid.\n\nFor the points to lie on the same straight line, they must either be in a row, a column, or on a diagonal.\n\n1. Row: There are 3 rows in the grid, and for each row, there are $\\binom{3}{3} = 1$ way to choose 3 points. So, there are a total of $3$ ways to choose 3 points in a row.\n\n2. Column: Similarly, there are 3 columns in the grid, and for each column, there are $\\binom{3}{3} = 1$ way to choose 3 points. So, there are a total of $3$ ways to choose 3 points in a column.\n\n3. Diagonal: There are 2 diagonals in the grid (main diagonal and anti-diagonal), and for each diagonal, there are $\\binom{3}{3} = 1$ way to choose 3 points. So, there are a total of $2$ ways to choose 3 points on a diagonal.\n\nTherefore, the total number of ways to choose 3 points that lie on the same straight line is $3 + 3 + 2 = 8$.\n\nThe probability that the points lie on the same straight line is given by the ratio of the number of favorable outcomes to the total number of outcomes, which is $\\frac{8}{84} = \\frac{2}{21}$.\n\nSo, the probability that the points lie on the same straight line is $\\boxed{\\frac{2}{21}}$.'
'The domain of the function $f(x) = \\frac{2-x}{\\log(2-\\log(x-2))}$ is $(2, 10)$, as stated in the hint. This means that $x$ can take any value between $2$ and $10$, but not including $2$ and $10$ themselves.\n\nTo find the number of real values of $x$ for which $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer, we need to consider the possible values of $x$ that make the expression inside the square root non-negative.\n\nGiven the expression $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$, the inner square root must be non-negative, so $120-\\sqrt{x} \\geq 0$. Solving this inequality, we get $\\sqrt{x} \\leq 120$, which implies $x \\leq 120^2 = 14400$.\n\nNext, we need to find the values of $x$ such that $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer within the range $0 \\leq x \\leq 14400$. From the hint, we know that $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer for perfect squares. Therefore, we need to find the perfect squares within the range $0 \\leq x \\leq 14400$.\n\nThe perfect squares within this range are $0, 1, 4, 9, 16, ..., 14400$. To find the number of perfect squares in this range, we can take the square root of the endpoints and count the integers in between. The square root of $0$ is $0$ and the square root of $14400$ is $120$, so there are $121$ perfect squares in this range.\n\nTherefore, there are $121$ real values of $x$ for which $\\sqrt{120-\\sqrt{x}}$ is an integer.'
'To find all possible values of $x$, we first square both sides of the equation $\\sqrt{3x-5}=2$ to eliminate the square root:\n\n$(\\sqrt{3x-5})^2 = 2^2$\n\n$3x-5 = 4$\n\n$3x = 9$\n\n$x = 3$\n\nTherefore, the only possible value of $x$ is $3$.\n\nTo find $P(16)$, we need to substitute $x=16$ into the expression. However, since we have already determined that the only possible value of $x$ is $3$, $P(16)$ is undefined.'
'To find the remainder when the $100^{\\text{th}}$ term of the Fibonacci sequence is divided by 4, we can first observe the pattern of remainders when each term is divided by 4.\n\nThe first few terms of the Fibonacci sequence modulo 4 are:\n1, 1, 2, 3, 1, 0, 1, 1, 2, 3, 1, 0, ...\n\nWe can see that the sequence repeats every 6 terms. Therefore, to find the remainder when the $100^{\\text{th}}$ term is divided by 4, we can find the remainder when $100$ is divided by 6.\n\n$100 \\div 6 = 16$ with a remainder of 4.\n\nTherefore, the $100^{\\text{th}}$ term of the Fibonacci sequence is the same as the $4^{\\text{th}}$ term, which is 3. So, the remainder when the $100^{\\text{th}}$ term is divided by 4 is $\\boxed{3}$.\n\nFor the second question, we are given the coordinates of three vertices of a parallelogram: (5, 3), (6, 8), and (7, 4). We need to find the coordinates of the fourth vertex, denoted as (x, y), with x > 7.\n\nSince opposite sides of a parallelogram are parallel, the vector connecting (5, 3) to (6, 8) is the same as the vector connecting (7, 4) to (x, y).\n\nThe vector from (5, 3) to (6, 8) is (1, 5). Therefore, the vector from (7, 4) to (x, y) must also be (1, 5).\n\nThis means that x = 7 + 1 = 8 and y = 4 + 5 = 9.\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of the fourth vertex are (8, 9).\n\nThe sum of x and y is $8 + 9 = \\boxed{17}$.'
